U.S. News: Lower Prices Help Lift Existing-Home Sales
By Justin Lahart
387 words
28 May 2009
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
(Copyright (c) 2009, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Existing-home sales rose last month, as distressed properties and low prices helped bolster the market.

The National Association of Realtors said Wednesday that sales of previously owned homes rose 2.9% in April, compared with March, to a 4.68 million annual rate. The increase nearly reversed March's decline, though the sales were 3.5% below the level in April 2008.

"Home sales keep chopping around," said J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. economist Michael Feroli. "We're seeing continued evidence that things are stable, albeit at very low levels."

Sales of distressed properties, such as homes in foreclosure, accounted for 45% of the April total, down from exactly half in March. Those sales are driving prices lower; the average selling price of $170,200 was 15.4% below the year-ago level.

The drop in home prices is aggravating many households' woes: Because the value of their homes has fallen below what they owe on their mortgage, they can't clear their debt by selling.

A separate government report suggests that the home-price declines may not be as severe as those shown by the National Association of Realtors, a trade association. The Federal Housing Finance Agency said its national housing-price index was 7.1% below its year-ago level in the first quarter, compared with the fourth quarter's year-over-year decline of 8.3%. Nevada registered the steepest decline, with prices falling 31.1%.

The Realtors' home-price measure can be distorted by the mix of homes sold in a month. If fewer high-end homes are sold, for example, that will drive the price measure down. The government measure avoids these problems by including only homes with financing from Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac. It also doesn't include homes with subprime mortgages, which have made up the bulk of the housing-market problems.

The separate S&P/Case-Shiller national home-price index, which fell 19.1% in the first quarter, avoids the distortions of the Realtors, and includes homes with subprime mortgages. But because it is based on prices in the 100 largest metropolitan areas, its scope isn't as wide as the government's.

Leading the News: Low prices help home resales rise 2.9% in U.S.
By Jeff Bater
445 words
28 May 2009
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
2
English
(c) 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

WASHINGTON -- Sales of existing homes in the U.S. climbed modestly in April as buyers took advantage of foreclosures and snatched up property carrying discounted price tags.

Home resales rose by 2.9% to a 4.68 million annual rate from 4.55 million in March, the National Association of Realtors said Wednesday. The NAR originally reported that March sales fell 3% to 4.57 million.

The April resales level was above Wall Street expectations of a 4.67 million sales rate for previously owned homes.

About 45% of the 4.68 million in April sales were foreclosures and "short sales" -- in which a homeowner sells the home for less than the amount owed on it. The large number of these distressed-property sales has driven prices lower, year over year. The median price for an existing home in the U.S. last month was $170,200, down 15% from $201,300 in April 2008.

Weak demand has kept inventories of unsold homes high. Inventories of previously owned homes jumped 8.8% at the end of April to 3.97 million available for sale. That represented a 10.2-month supply at the current sales pace, compared with 9.6 in March. Bloated inventories, in turn, are depressing prices.

"Most of the sales are taking place in lower price ranges and activity is beginning to pick up in the midprice ranges, but high-end home sales remain sluggish," NAR economist Lawrence Yun said.

Lower prices, combined with historically low borrowing costs, have increased affordability. The average 30-year mortgage rate was 4.81% in April, down from 5% in March, Freddie Mac data show. Realtors also hope demand is stirred by the $8,000 tax credit for first-time home buyers included in the Obama administration's economic-stimulus package.

A decline in prices is troubling because it can give pause to would-be buyers who hope for a better deal. On a bright note, the median price, on a monthly basis, has climbed for three months in a row. It was $169,900 in March, a downward revision from an originally reported $175,200.

Working against sales are tighter mortgage-lending standards, which have made it harder to finance a purchase. And the uncertain economic outlook is hurting the existing-home market. The U.S. unemployment rate rose to 8.9% in April from 8.5% in March.

Sales of previously owned homes, year over year, were down 3.5% from April 2008.

Real Estate
In Commercial-Property Market, Hope for Revival Takes Hit; S&P Warning on CMBS Ratings Undermines TALF's Buying Plan -- and the Securities' Underlying Assets
By Lingling Wei
1075 words
27 May 2009
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
C8
English
Copyright 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Lingling WeiMarket enthusiasm for the Federal Reserve's plan to help the commercial-real-estate market was doused Tuesday after Standard & Poor's warned that billions of dollars of top-rated bonds backed by commercial mortgages could face downgrades.

The financing drought in commercial real estate is threatening to cause major damage to the economy, so the sector was cheered when the Fed announced in March that it would expand one of its main rescue programs to help resuscitate the $700 billion market for bonds backed by mortgages on office towers, strip malls and other commercial property. The market for commercial mortgage-backed securities, or CMBS, rallied on the news.

But the Standard & Poor's action has raised doubts about how effective the Fed program will be. Investors responded Tuesday by selling off CMBS, with yields on triple-A securities widening to 10.7% from 9.4% one week ago, according to data tracker Trepp.

The Fed program, called the Term Asset-Backed Securities Loan Facility, or TALF, is designed to jump-start lending by increasing investor demand for securities tied to all kinds of assets, including commercial real estate. So far, the $1 trillion program has been made available only for consumer debt, such as credit-card loans. The program makes available inexpensive loans to investors buying these securities.

TALF will soon start offering this low-cost, nonrecourse financing to investors to purchase CMBS. But under the Fed's rules, only the highest rated CMBS are eligible. That is why S&P's action sparked so much consternation. The rating agency announced proposed changes to its CMBS rating method that would likely lead to substantial downgrades on triple-A-rated bonds issued from 2005 through 2007. That would make them ineligible for TALF.

S&P said the loans made during those years featured "increasingly more aggressive underwriting" than those made in prior years. In other words, lenders were overly optimistic about rents, occupancies and values. In fact, they have all fallen sharply.

Policy makers have been worried that without TALF, banks and other financial institutions may suffer hundreds of billions of dollars worth of losses from value declines and defaults on commercial-real-estate loans. "This program is critical to restoring the flow of credit to owners of commercial real estate and preventing a damaging chain of events in this market," Treasury Secretary Timothy Geithner said in recent testimony.

The TALF program has two parts to help commercial real estate: One to create a market for existing CMBS; the other to unfreeze the market for new CMBS issues. Wall Street sold $230 billion of CMBS in 2007, a record year, compared with $10 billion in 2008 and zero so far this year.

A number of banks including Deutsche Bank AG and J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. have been working on new CMBS deals that could be eligible for TALF financing, according to people familiar with the matter. Representatives at Deutsche Bank and J.P. Morgan declined to comment.

Some borrowers are gearing up, too. Developers Diversified Realty Corp., an owner of 710 shopping centers globally, is preparing to be among the first real-estate-investment trusts to borrow against properties through the program. Developers Diversified is assembling a pool of at least $750 million in shopping centers to be used as collateral for a CMBS deal of $250 million to $300 million, says David Oakes, the REIT's chief investment officer. "Just to get the market started again is very important to the industry overall," Mr. Oakes says.

But owners of highly leveraged property likely won't be able to take advantage of the rekindled CMBS market, at least for now. The first few TALF-eligible CMBS deals should be backed by "very conservative loans," says Hugh Balloch, managing principal at Goff Capital Partners LP, a Stamford, Conn., private-equity firm specializing in commercial-real-estate investments. That would leave highly levered borrowers to have to find ways to "fill that gap with equity" or face potential foreclosures, Mr. Balloch said.

The Fed, mindful of protecting taxpayers' interests, has set high bars for the type of loan it will accept as eligible for TALF financing. Among those that won't qualify: floating-rate mortgages, construction loans or loans secured by properties that don't have a steady cash flow, according to the Fed.

In addition, instead of solely relying on rating agencies, the Fed also will hire firms in the private sector to help it screen the quality of the loans. A handful of firms will be hired within the next few weeks to serve as collateral monitors for the Fed, people familiar with the issue said.

Richard Parkus, head of CMBS research at Deutsche Bank, estimates at least two-thirds, or $410 billion, of the CMBS loans maturing from this year to 2018 wouldn't be able to refinance without more cash put up by the property owners.

Meanwhile, other hurdles also exist for a return of the CMBS market involving the so-called B-piece of CMBS issues, bankers and investors caution. Typically when banks slice commercial mortgages into bonds, the tranches first in line to suffer losses in a default are ones rated single-B by rating firms that also pay the highest interest rate.

The TALF program is only financing investors' purchases of the top tranche, triple-A-rated bonds. That means banks may still have a hard time getting investors to buy B-piece debt with no government-sponsored financing available. Some market participants also worry that potential B-piece buyers may be discouraged because, under the TALF funding terms, it is unclear if B-piece buyers would have control over how to work out a loan with a borrower in the event of default.

The key question for a B-piece buyer is "how do you avoid having 25% of your class wiped out" in a default situation, said Eric Hillenbrand, chief executive of Hillenbrand Partners in Chicago. His firm, which specializes in buying B-piece debt, is talking to some financial institutions about structuring a CMBS deal to take advantage of the TALF financing. The potential size of the deal: $300 million to $500 million.

Kris Hudson contributed to this article.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Personal Finance
Should You Buy a Home Equity Protection Plan?
By Lisa Scherzer
Smart Money
920 words
26 May 2009
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Watching a home's value plunge by double-digit percentages in a matter of months is enough to unnerve even the most financially secure homeowner. And, as the real estate market continues to reel concerns are growing that the free fall is far from over.

Playing into that anxiety are two companies – EquityLock Financial, based in Austin, Texas, and Lighthouse Group based in Charlotte, N.C. – that are selling products promising to put a little more control in homeowners' hands. Their pitch? That homeowners could spend a little now to hedge against declines in the value of their home later.

Here's how it works: For a fee of 1% to 3% of their home's value, homeowners buy a contract that protects them against the loss of equity in their home if the market takes a turn for the worse. The contract, which should not be confused with an insurance policy, pays the homeowner when he sells his home in a market where average home prices have dropped since their purchase. The amount he receives is tied to the size of the market's decline, as measured by one of two home price indexes (both of which are based on sales of single-family homes).

Say you buy a home in Denver for $300,000. Five years later, after Denver's home price index falls 10%, you sell it for $290,000. At closing, the company you bought the equity protection from pays you $30,000 – your original purchase price times 10%. Even if you sold your home for more than what you paid to buy it, you can still make the claim – as long as the index fell. (If the index rises, however, you can't make a claim and you're out the $6,000 you spent buying the contract.)

"It's the kind of product that ends up being useful for a niche part of the population, but likely not really worthwhile for homeowners in general," says Todd Sinai, associate professor of real estate at the Wharton School.

One factor to consider is how long you plan to live in your home. If it's going to be 10 or 15 years, buying protection doesn't make much sense, says Susan Wachter, real estate professor at Wharton. While home prices can change dramatically in the short term, they hold steady and increase over longer periods. For short-term homeowners, though, it might make more sense. If, for instance, you anticipate a move for work and worry that area prices will sag, the protection can be worthwhile, says Sinai.

We spoke with real estate experts about what to look out for when shopping for home equity protection plans. Here's what they had to say:

Calculate the costs

Payment terms are different for each product. EquityLock's premium, which is paid upfront, ranges from 1% to 3% of the purchase price (or of the current value if you already own a home). Lighthouse's premium could be paid either on a monthly or yearly basis, and typically works out to just under 1% of the home's value, per year, says a company spokesman.

Say your house is worth $300,000 and you pay $6,000 (a 2% premium) for EquityLock's protection. You're starting with a $6,000 loss, and you'll only break even if the house index drops enough from the time you purchase the home to the time you decide to sell. "You really have to think hard if the initial cost is worth it," says Sinai.

Watch out for lockout periods

Be sure to ask about any lockout period which bars you from collecting payment before a set time. EquityLock imposes an exclusion term ranging from 18 to 30 months from the time the protection is purchased. So you can't sell your home and make a claim a year after the contract begins. Lighthouse has no lockout period. But if a homeowner exercises the contract after, say, the first month, they still owe the future monthly payments that are due.

Housing indexes are imperfect

Potential payouts are tied to a house price index: either the S&P Case-Shiller house price index, which tracks 20 metro markets, or the Federal Housing Finance Agency's House Price Index, commonly known as OFHEO, which covers 386 markets. The problem? Indexes don't capture the true volatility and variation within markets – and don't always correlate with the specific value of your home, says Jonathan Adams, a finance professor at NYU. For example, if highway construction has just gotten underway at the next block over, chances are your home's value would dive – but the index might stay the same, says Adams. (Lighthouse says tying the contract to an index is the most efficient way to determine a home's value, without having to actually sell the house or get an appraisal.)

Be wary of new, untested products

Bear in mind the novelty of these plans when assessing their worth. "When it comes to finances and your home equity, you really want to go with someone who has a history," says Alison Southwick, spokeswoman for the Better Business Bureau. Also, new products tend to be more expensive, says Mark Browne, professor of risk management at the University of Wisconsin. They haven't been around for long, so it might be better to wait for competition to build.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Markets
Housing-Starts Data Keep Lid on Stocks
By Peter A. McKay and Rob Curran
470 words
20 May 2009
05:33
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
C1
English
Copyright 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Stocks churned to a mixed finish on Tuesday after a surprisingly weak report on the housing market failed to puncture investors' optimism about an impending economic recovery.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average declined 29.23 points, or 0.3%, at 8474.85, weighed down by a slide of 5.3% for Home Depot after the home-improvement giant posted stronger-than-expected earnings but said that its sales and margins declined. The chain reaffirmed its guidance for the year ahead.

An upbeat earnings outlook from Home Depot rival Lowe's stirred hopes of an imminent turnaround for the housing market on Monday, but that thesis took a knock on Tuesday when data showed housing starts unexpectedly fell in April.

A large decline in apartment groundbreakings offset a modest increase in new single-family construction, causing housing starts to fall 12.8% compared to the prior month, the Commerce Department reported. But some economists said that the increased building of single-family homes is a positive sign.

"Since this area is where most of the housing problems originated, ongoing though slow healing here is a positive step," economists at Goldman Sachs said.

The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 2.18 points, or 0.13%, to close at 1734.54. The S&P 500-stock index slipped 1.58 points, or 0.2%, to 908.13.

Another promising sign was a retreat for the Chicago Board Options Exchange Volatility Index push below 30 for the first time since September. The VIX had indicated that even buyers of stocks were willing to pay lofty premiums to hedge their bets on the options market ever since September. It ended down more than 5% at 28.66.

Other stress indicators eased. The spread between the London interbank offered rate and yields on Treasury bonds has continued to retreat from its peak late last year. That shows that money is flowing around the banking system with ease.

"We're just not seeing the volatility of forced selling right now and that creates a ton of positives for the market," said Shawn Price, a portfolio manager with Navellier.

Most traders and analysts remain cautiously bullish on stocks, based in part on the idea that pressure is rising for investors sitting on the sidelines to put to work their excess cash. That money is garnering little interest because of the Federal Reserve's rock-bottom target intended to spur an economic recovery.

Crude-oil futures briefly moved over $60 a barrel to hit a six-month high. The front-month contract settled up 62 cents, or 1.1%, at $59.65 a barrel.

Economy &Politics
U.S. housing starts decline amid pullback in apartments
By Jeff Bater
275 words
20 May 2009
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
9
English
(c) 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

WASHINGTON -- U.S. home construction unexpectedly fell in April, toppled by a sharp drop in apartment starts that cast a shadow over signs of stabilization in housing.

Housing starts decreased 12.8% to a seasonally adjusted 458,000 annual rate compared with the prior month, the Commerce Department said Tuesday. Starts fell 8.5% in March.

Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires forecast a 2% increase in April starts.

Leading the fall, construction of housing with two or more units dropped 46.1% as developers struggled to get financing. Single-family housing starts, though, rose 2.8%.

"We still have a large glut of homes for sale, particularly used ones," Weiss Research analyst Mike Larson said. "But these figures add to the evidence of potential stabilization in that part of the industry."

There have been other indications the housing crisis might be nearing an end. New-home sales, for instance, are up from a January low. Builder confidence in the housing market is growing. The National Association of Home Builders' latest Housing Market Index rose to 16 in May from 14 in April and nine in March.

"It increasingly looks like January was the bottom for home sales [new and existing], home builder confidence, and single-family housing starts," wrote Michael Darda, chief economist at MKM Partners.

Yet, the job market is very weak, credit is tight and inventories of homes are high. People are buying foreclosed existing homes for a discount, rather than new homes.

U.S. News: Bright Spot in Down Housing Data
By Justin Lahart
469 words
20 May 2009
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
(Copyright (c) 2009, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

New-home construction in the U.S. fell to a new low last month. But an increase in the construction of single-family homes suggests the slump in home building is drawing to a close.

In April, the pace of construction starts fell to an annual rate of 458,000 new homes, a 12.8% drop from March. That was the lowest level since 1959, when the Commerce Department began tracking the figures.

The decline was wholly the result of a sharp drop in ground-breaking for apartment buildings and other multifamily dwellings. Single-family home construction rose 2.8% to 368,000 in April, after rising slightly in March as well.

The increase in the single-family figures adds to evidence that residential construction has begun to recover. The National Association of Home Builders said Monday that its measure of home-builder sentiment increased for a second month in May. New-home sales appear to be turning upward.

Steve Temkin, owner of T&M Building in Torrington, Conn., has started to see business pick up. After weekends when hardly anyone came by, foot traffic in his model homes is back up, and he just made the first two sales in nearly a year in one of his new projects. To adjust to the weak market, he is building smaller homes starting at about $220,000 -- in places where prices once started at more than $350,000.

"We keep tweaking our designs to see what we can come up with that will attract a different crowd," Mr. Temkin said.

Many economists believe the single-family construction figures are the best measure by which to gauge the housing market. They say builders breaking ground for new homes is the most reliable sign that mortgage woes are getting resolved, that prices have fallen to appropriate levels and that the glut of unsold homes has thinned.

Even though housing may have turned the corner, that doesn't herald a robust recovery in a market where financing is difficult to come by, and many homes continue to bear "for sale" signs.

"There are still huge inventories of vacant homes on the market, so there's still going to be some pressure," said James O'Sullivan, an economist at UBS AG.

With rents falling and financing tight, the number of multifamily-home starts fell 46% to 90,000. Regionally, overall new-home construction fell 21.1% in the South, 21.4% in the Midwest and 30.6% in the Northeast, while jumping 42.5% in the West. The number of building permits issued in April fell 3.3% to a 494,000 annual rate, but permits for single-family homes rose 3.6%.

Homebuilders Woo Renters
By Dawn Wotapka
368 words
20 May 2009
The Wall Street Journal
J
English
(Copyright (c) 2009, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

To turn renters into homeowners, home builders are getting creative. They're gathering addresses to contact tenants, participating in rent-with-equity programs and wooing them with pet adoption drives.

It's all part of the broader strategy to boost anemic sales as the sector limps through its worst downturn in decades. "The tougher the market gets, the more back to basics we get," said Dan Klinger, president of K. Hovnanian American Mortgage, the lending subsidiary of builder Hovnanian Enterprises. "And soliciting renters is about as basic as it comes."

First-time buyers not saddled with an existing home to sell have become critical for a recovery, pushing builders to design smaller and more affordable homes. And they're racing the clock: A federal tax credit of up to $8,000 for qualified first-timers expires Dec. 1, while funds for California's credit of up to $10,000 for buyers of new homes could be exhausted soon.

Builders are accentuating very low interest rates and more affordable prices that may make owning cheaper than renting. Builders face hurdles including fears among home buyers that prices have further to fall, job insecurity and the inability to secure credit.

Mid-America Apartment Communities said recently that the number of renters leaving their properties to buy a home in the first quarter fell by 31% compared with a year earlier.

Still, with nearly 89 million renters nationwide, there may be potential to sell the 340,000 single-family homes that will be started this year, estimates residential research firm Metrostudy.

"I think it's a large untapped market," said Jeffrey T. Mezger, KB Home's president and chief executive.

In its effort to woo renters and emphasize affordability, KB Home, the nation's fifth-largest builder by annual closings, redid its Web site to highlight monthly payments over the total price.

The builder also offers more flexible and personalized floor plans.

"Our sales are up and our first-time buyer sales are up," Mr. Mezger said. In the first quarter 70% of deliveries went to first-time buyers, compared with 53% a year ago, he said.

Bright Spot in Down Housing Data
By Justin Lahart
469 words

U.S. News: Home Builders' Optimism Edged Higher Again in May
By Michael Corkery
286 words
19 May 2009
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
(Copyright (c) 2009, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

In another sign that the market for new homes may be nearing a bottom, home builders became more optimistic about sales conditions this month, according to a trade group's monthly measure of builder sentiment.

The National Association of Home Builders' housing-market index rose for the second consecutive month, as new-home sales continued to benefit from falling prices, an $8,000 tax credit for certain first-time home buyers and various state incentives for home buyers.

The index, which gauges current and future sales conditions, rose two points to 16 in May, after a five point increase in April.

"While the home-builder index is still at an extremely low level, its increase over the last couple of months suggests that new single-family home sales hit bottom early in the year and are now slowly increasing," said J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. economist Abiel Reinhart in a research note.

The rising index helped push home builders' shares sharply higher on Monday, with Beazer Homes USA Inc. rising 20% to $2.81. Shares of Lennar Corp. jumped 14% to $10.02 after Citigroup analyst Josh Levin upgraded the Miami-based builder to "buy" on signs that sales in May are "off to a good start" and in a few cases builders have reported a "modicum of pricing power."

Large home builders, such as Lennar, also have been able to raise some cash in recent months by successfully selling debt. However, the industry still faces stiff competition for its new homes from discounted foreclosed homes. Credit for home buyers also remains tight.

Real Estate
Home Builders' Optimism Edged Higher Again in May
By Michael Corkery
287 words
18 May 2009
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

In another sign that the market for new homes may be nearing a bottom, home builders became more optimistic about sales conditions this month, according to a trade group's monthly measure of builder sentiment.

The National Association of Home Builders' housing-market index rose for the second consecutive month, as new-home sales continued to benefit from falling prices, an $8,000 tax credit for certain first-time home buyers and various state incentives for home buyers.

The index, which gauges current and future sales conditions, rose two points to 16 in May, after a five point increase in April.

"While the home-builder index is still at an extremely low level, its increase over the last couple of months suggests that new single-family home sales hit bottom early in the year and are now slowly increasing," said J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. economist Abiel Reinhart in a research note.

Shares of Lennar Corp. jumped 14% to $10.02 after Citigroup analyst Josh Levin upgraded the Miami-based builder to "buy" on signs that sales in May are "off to a good start" and in a few cases builders have reported a "modicum of pricing power."

Large home builders, such as Lennar, also have been able to raise some cash in recent months by successfully selling debt. However, the industry still faces stiff competition for its new homes from discounted foreclosed homes. Credit for home buyers also remains tight.

Special
If you're thinking of retiring in... Beauty and the Beach; The scenic towns of Guilford and Madison are luring retirees to the Connecticut coast
By Riva Richmond
1722 words
16 May 2009
The Wall Street Journal (Online and Print)
WSJO
In-Depth Reports
English
Copyright 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

(See Corrections &Amplifications item below.)

John and Barbara Henningson plotted their retirement on the Connecticut coast for years.

The New York couple harbored fond memories from early in their marriage of vacations at a summer home in Madison, Conn., once owned by Ms. Henningson's parents. The pair's wish list included a slower pace, boating and small-town community life—a sharp contrast from 10 years of commuting between a weekday apartment in Manhattan and a suburban home in Westchester County in New York. And they wanted to remain in the Northeast near their son and grandchildren.

So nearly 10 years before Mr. Henningson, an environmental engineer, retired in 2003, the couple began to search for the right waterfront property and community on the stretch of rocky coastline between Milford and Old Lyme. In 1997, they bought a weathered, gray cottage, built in 1902, in the Sachem's Head area of Guilford; the next year, they began spending weekends there. When Ms. Henningson, a financial comptroller at Citigroup, retired in 2005, the two sold their Manhattan apartment and settled in Guilford for good (though they do escape to Florida in the dead of winter).

Now the Henningsons are fully immersed in their new life. Like many old New England towns, Guilford has a town-meeting form of government that depends largely on volunteers. Mr. Henningson lends his expertise as chairman of the town's harbor-management commission and writes news releases for Faulkner's Light Brigade, which helps maintain the historic 1802 Faulkner's Island Lighthouse and its eroding island. Ms. Henningson recently became an elected member of Guilford's board of assessment appeals (an unpaid position), after sitting on the town's economic-development and ethics committees. She is also vice president of the Sachem's Head neighborhood association and active in the Garden Club, which maintains flowers about town.

All Inclusive

"New York was never a community," Ms. Henningson says. "If you're interested and you're willing to spend the time, [Guilford is] a very inclusive community. We've made a wonderful circle of friends."

Indeed, retirees to Guilford and neighboring towns like Madison are often transplants from New York City and its environs. Some kept a summer home in the area and then chose to settle full time in retirement. Some are moving closer to family, and others are refugees of the more costly and congested New York bedroom communities of Connecticut's Fairfield County, such as Westport and Greenwich, or Westchester County, as in the case of the Henningsons.

Retirees who once might have moved to Florida or Arizona are making decisions based more on family and social connections, and thus are finding some appealing options in their backyards, says Steve J. Maun, president of Leyland Alliance LLC, a New York firm that plans to build an active-adult neighborhood on the waterfront in Madison.

"There's a very strong trend where people want to keep their existing social connections," he says. What's more, they're eschewing the isolation and car-culture of suburbia and rural areas for a more urban life, Mr. Maun notes—a setting in which home-maintenance chores can be minimized, and "they can get a cup of coffee and meet a neighbor."

Guilford and Madison are particularly attractive because, unlike many other Connecticut towns, they have retained quaint town centers. Guilford's pride is its old Town Green, where the annual Christmas-tree lighting draws crowds, as do an art fair, free concerts and Shakespeare productions in summer. Surrounding the green are a few boutiques, a coffeehouse and hardware store, as well as Town Hall, several churches and Colonial-era buildings. Madison's Main Street is treasured for its art cinema and an independent bookshop that plays host to top authors and bejewels its heavy wooden cases with staff-scrawled book recommendations.

Towns and Country

The towns are also conveniently located between New York and Boston, both reached by train or car in about two hours. And they're a stone's throw from New Haven, home to Yale University's intellectual community and well-regarded hospital as well as cultural attractions like the Shubert Theater and many restaurants.

Yet despite the proximity to these cities, both Guilford and Madison have managed to preserve much of their natural beauty and historic character. Here, prim 19th-century wood-shuttered homes overlook salt marshes, blue-gray seas or winding wooded roads. Favorite pastimes are hiking in the woods, all-season strolls along the beach and, of course, for sailors and fishermen, taking out the boat. Locals pick their own apples, pears and peaches at Bishop's Orchards—one of the area's few remaining working family farms—as well as hunt, clam and fish for oysters.

"It's all these traditional and old-world things that go into making us special," says Carl Balestracci, Guilford's first selectman. Some towns in New England are governed by boards of selectmen, whose top vote-winner takes on a role similar to that of mayor.

Search for a Sunset

For Jean-Marc and Annick Pandraud, it was the sunset on the water that sold them on Guilford. The French couple, who live in Lexington, Mass., had been mulling whether to stay in the U.S. or to retire in France. They have two daughters in U.S. colleges and one still in high school. If they were going to move elsewhere in the U.S., Ms. Pandraud had one requirement: a view with sunset over water, something she had loved in France.

That's a tall order on the East Coast of the U.S. But Mr. Pandraud, 55, who recently retired as chief operating officer of technology company Entegris Inc., contacted a slew of real-estate agents along the Connecticut and Rhode Island coastline in search of the prize. He found it in Guilford, where from a tip of land in Long Island Sound there are sunsets over the ocean when you look southwest in winter. To get the view, the Pandrauds had to buy a summer house in need of winterization, but they aim to move in permanently in the next year and a half.

By coincidence, "we found a fantastic community," Mr. Pandraud says. "When you are there after five minutes, you are already in a different world."

Judy Whitehead, 68, was lured to Madison by her niece and Hammonasset Beach State Park. She had been living in central Connecticut, where she worked as a metallurgist for United Technologies Corp. until she retired at 55. But when her husband died three years ago, she wanted to shed their big house and couple-centered social life and start anew.

So she bought a condominium within walking distance of the beach, where she and her husband spent many Saturdays as teenagers before they married. "It took me about 20 minutes to look at the condo and sign the papers on the hood of the real-estate agent's car," Ms. Whitehead says. "I always loved the area."

Now, she gets her daily exercise, weather permitting, with a walk on the beach, 4½ miles up and back. She has also built a busy social life with lots of women friends by joining two book clubs, a garden club and the American Association of University Women, among other groups.

Home Economics

For the most part, it helps to have a good-size wallet to settle along the Connecticut coast—and a good coat. The median price of a home in 2008 was $425,000 in Guilford and $539,500 in Madison, according to the Connecticut Economic Resource Center, a state agency. Winter temperatures regularly dip into the low 20s.

Ms. Whitehead says the biggest drawback of living in Madison is also a positive—she can't reach a big-box store without driving at least 40 minutes. Indeed, Madison and Guilford, both wealthy towns, have often demonstrated a willingness to bear the price of preserving their characters. They have kept commercial development to a minimum, and, as such, most of the tax burden rests on residential-property owners. Still, residents say tax rates are lower than in New York, for example.

"We aren't interested in attracting business and industry," says Madison First Selectman Alfred Goldberg. Homeowners bear 92% of the tax burden and industry only 8%. "That has been a conscious decision."

The towns also aim to protect and expand public green space. Guilford recently negotiated the purchase of 624 acres from a local family for $14.4 million, which will be paid for by selling town bonds offset somewhat by $3 million from the federal government. The property, which includes two miles of riverfront land along the East River and a tidal marsh rich with wildlife, will become a nature preserve with public hiking trails.

Madison is in the midst of a development controversy over Leyland Alliance's active-adult project, Madison Landing, which has faced resistance among some townspeople. The land, once a private airport, is the last large parcel on Madison's shoreline and is contiguous with Hammonasset Beach State Park. The town has asked Trust for Public Land, a nonprofit skilled at negotiating deals and raising money, to work with it on open-space issues and to consider the possibility of making an offer for the property.

Leyland says it has held "exploratory" talks with the trust but maintains the land isn't for sale. It has already gotten most of the clearances needed for the project and plans to break ground in the first quarter of 2010 and complete construction of 127 units—a mix of apartments, townhouses and single-family homes priced between $500,000 and $1 million—within three or four years. The company has pledged to create a nature walk open to the public.

-- Ms. Richmond is a writer in New York. She can be reached at encore@wsj.com [mailto:encore@wsj.com].

Real Estate
Should Hedge Funder Park Savings in San Francisco Real Estate? Our columnist advises a renter with $1.5 million saved.
By June Fletcher
797 words
14 May 2009
11:10
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

(See Corrections & Amplification item below[correction])

Q. I'm 31-year-old lifelong renter. From working for a hedge fund for the last four years, I have $1.5 million in municipal bonds, the stock market, certificates of deposit and cash, and no hard assets other than my car. But considering the current environment for hedge funds, I foresee losing my job, which pays $150,000 annually. I'm thinking about purchasing real estate, so I can take advantage of mortgage tax breaks and stop paying rent. I'm thinking of buying about $2 million worth of property, possibly a single-family home and a multifamily unit, using about $600,000 for down payments. Do you think this is wise?

A. Congratulations on having the discipline to save such a large amount of money in such a short time, and for investing it widely. Since real estate can help you hedge against downswings in these other investment classes--at least if you invest for the long term--I think you're wise to look into it.

But since you don't feel your job is secure, you may be better off if you continue renting for now--or at least scale down your buying ambitions--for several reasons.

First, you can deduct mortgage interest on your federal tax bill, but only for the first $1 million in home acquisition debt. Second, real estate is relatively illiquid in the best of times, and we are in the worst of times. Right now, should you lose your job suddenly and have to relocate, you could just end your lease and move. If you own one or more properties, you may have to sell at a loss or become a long-distance landlord, with all the stress, hassle and expense that this entails.

Then there is the gap between the cost of renting and owning in San Francisco, which has been wide for many years. As a tenant, you're benefiting from this gap; as an owner, you lose out. For instance, on Trulia.com[http://Trulia.com], I recently saw a one-bedroom loft in the SoMa district listed for $499,000.

I know you can afford to pay cash for such a property, but for the sake of comparison of owning and renting, let's assume you bought it as most buyers would, with 10% down, at 5% interest, with $1,000 annual tax and $300 annual insurance. Your monthly payment would be $2,519, but you'd only be able to command a rent of about $2,500 a month for the property, according to Zilpy.com[http://Zilpy.com], a Web site that uses nearby comps to estimate rental returns for specific properties.

Meanwhile, you can't count on appreciation to make up the difference. Property prices have fallen precipitously in San Francisco since the housing bubble burst, and many economists think the city won't start to recover until late this year or early next year. According to the latest Standard & Poor's/Case Shiller[http://online.wsj.com/article/SB124092346703363431.html#project%3DCASE_SHILLER090428%26articleTabs%3Dinteractive] home price index, in February existing single-family prices fell 31%, and condos 23%, from a year earlier.

Nevertheless, if you're sure you will stay in the area for the next few years, you might want to invest in real estate anyway. But, cautious and don't take on too much debt, considering your shaky job situation. I don't think you should buy $2 million worth of properties without feeling secure that your current income will continue or if you don't have much hope for positive cash flow or appreciation in the current economy. If something goes wrong, you'd soon have to raid your other savings to pay your hefty mortgage payments, even if you put $600,000 down.

Instead, I'd suggest that you buy a $500,000 to $600,000 foreclosed or short-sale duplex. Either pay cash, or finance the purchase with a down payment of at least 20% to avoid paying private mortgage insurance. These days, many such properties can be purchased at a huge discount over traditional sales, giving you instant equity and cushioning you against potential home price declines. Plus, you'll eliminate your rental payment and get some income from your tenant, making it less likely that you'll have to dip into your savings should you lose your job.

Write to June Fletcher at fletcher.june@gmail.com[mailto:fletcher.june@gmail.com]

The monthly payments on $499,000 home at a 5% interest rate with 10% down, would be $2,519. The original version of this article said that monthly cost was $2,523. We regret the error.

U.S. News: U.S. Median House Price Declines 14%
By James R. Hagerty
365 words
13 May 2009
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
(Copyright (c) 2009, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The median price for a single-family house fell 14% to $169,000 in the first quarter from a year earlier, the National Association of Realtors reported.

The trade group said first-time home buyers accounted for half of all purchases in the quarter, and many of them zeroed in on foreclosed homes. That dragged down the median, the Realtors said.

The median price for the latest quarter is down 26% from a peak of $227,600 in the third quarter of 2005. The latest median price was down from a year earlier in 134 of the 152 metro areas included in the survey.

The biggest increase was in the Cumberland area of Maryland and West Virginia, where the median price climbed 21% to $114,900. Debbie Grimm, manager of the Long &Foster real-estate brokerage in Cumberland, Md., said the area is attracting retirees and second-home buyers, particularly from Washington and Baltimore.

The lowest median price among the metro areas was $30,300 in Saginaw, Mich., and the highest was $570,000 in Honolulu. Most of the areas with the lowest prices are in troubled parts of the industrial Midwest. But a glut of homes in Cape Coral-Fort Myers, Fla., pushed the median down 59% from a year earlier to $87,300 -- ranking it just below Gary, Ind., which, at $92,000, was down 26%.

Sales of single-family homes and condominiums declined 6.8% from a year earlier to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.6 million units. But sales were up sharply in some areas hardest hit by the housing bust, largely because bargain hunters were out in force. States with big sales increases from the depressed levels of a year before included Nevada (up 117%), California (81%) and Arizona (50%) and Florida (25%).

Rising unemployment and fears of more job losses are deterring many potential buyers. But others are encouraged by the lowest mortgage rates since the 1950s. In addition, the U.S. government is offering tax credits for certain home buyers before Dec. 1.

Personal Finance
April Drop In Listings For Homes
By James R. Hagerty
466 words
12 May 2009
The Wall Street Journal
J
D3
English
(Copyright (c) 2009, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The number of homes listed for sale in many U.S. cities continued to fall in April in what some analysts see as a sign that the market may be nearing a bottom. But the picture is clouded by uncertainty over how many foreclosed properties will hit the market.

The supply of homes for sale in 29 major metropolitan areas at the end of April was down 3.6% from a month earlier, according to figures compiled by ZipRealty Inc., a real-estate brokerage firm based in Emeryville, Calif. The ZipRealty data cover all single-family homes, condominiums and town houses listed on local multiple-listing services in metro areas where the firm operates.

On a national basis, inventories typically increase in April as for-sale signs bloom for the spring home-shopping season. Since 1982, the average increase in April from the prior month has been 4.8%, according to Zelman &Associates, a research firm.

Compared with the year-earlier month, the April inventory in the 29 metro areas was down 21%.

The National Association of Realtors says about 3.7 million homes were listed for sale nationwide at the end of March, down 10% from a year earlier. But that number doesn't include all of the foreclosed homes that banks aim to sell.

Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, among the biggest owners of foreclosed homes, typically have only about 35% to 50% of those homes listed for sale at any given time, according to industry estimates. At one of the nation's largest banks, about 60% of the foreclosed homes are listed, according to a person familiar with the situation. Some foreclosed homes aren't listed because they are being offered for rental; others are awaiting repairs or are subject to litigation or other delays.

Tom Lawler, a housing economist based in Leesburg, Va., says the decline in listings "suggests that the bottom in home prices is much closer than many pundits believe." He also notes that housing construction has plunged. But he cautions that it's unclear how many foreclosed homes will go on the market in the months ahead.

Barclays Capital estimates that banks and loan investors owned 765,500 foreclosed homes as of April 1, up from 629,100 a year earlier. Barclays forecasts that this inventory will peak at around 1.3 million homes in mid- to late-2010.

The Zip data don't cover New York City. But Miller Samuel Inc., an appraisal firm there, reports there were 10,369 cooperative apartments and condominiums on the market in Manhattan at the end of April. That was down 0.7% from March but up 20% from April 2008.

Los Angeles Office Sale May Be a Loss Leader, if It Can Set a Bottom
By Maura Webber Sadovi
845 words
6 May 2009
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
C8
English
Copyright 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

In the dark and stormy commercial real-estate sales market, a deal involving a 42-story office tower in downtown Los Angeles may finally shine some light on how far values have fallen.

Viewed as one of the city's premier office buildings, the 992,000-square-foot glass-clad One California Plaza in the tony Bunker Hill neighborhood was put on the block in January by Macquarie Office Trust, a battered Australia-based real estate investment trust.

The property has attracted interest from a wide range of investors, but many of them lobbed in lowball bids. Others, like New York-based Emmes Group of Cos. and San Francisco-based Shorenstein Properties LLC have window-shopped but to date have passed on bidding, according to people familiar with the matter. Meanwhile, the L.A. buzz is that CB Richard Ellis Investors, Los Angeles-based CommonWealth Partners and San Francisco's DivcoWest are also in the mix.

If there is a deal, brokers believe it will be in the $200 million to $240 million range. Macquarie purchased an 80% stake in the building in 2006 in a deal that valued the property at $325 million. The seller was Los Angeles-based Maguire Properties, which retained ownership of 20% of the tower.

Since there have been so few sales, the price of One California would be closely scrutinized as a harbinger of the losses sellers will need to take if they want to get cash out of prime downtown properties. While a few high-profile office buildings in other cities have sold, some for as little as half of their peak value, no large office buildings in downtown L.A. have traded hands this year, according to Real Capital Analytics, a New York-based research firm. "Everybody's starved ... for people to actually buy," says Whitley Collins, senior managing director with Jones Lang LaSalle in Los Angeles. "Whether this is a bottom or not, people will feel better knowing there's some activity."

A price of $240 million would mean a so-called capitalization rate in the 7.5% to 8% range, according to Michael Knott, a senior analyst at Green Street Advisors in Newport Beach, Calif. That is a closely watched metric used to value buildings derived by dividing a building's annual net operating income by the price paid. They have been on the rise nationwide in part because buyers can no longer assume rents will rise as they did at the market's peak. Deals completed in downtown L.A. at the market's peak saw cap rates in the low 5% range, Mr. Knott says.

Designed by Arthur Erickson Architects and completed in 1985, One California has issues. It is only about 76% leased and its vacancies could grow as a number of leases are set to expire in the next few years, according to CoStar Group Inc. Bank of the West, which occupies about 78,625 square feet of office space, is mulling options that include moving out of the building or staying on when its lease expires next year. The property also carries a mortgage that matures late next year.

The outlook for the Los Angeles's downtown office market, popular with law firms and financial-service companies now facing pressures, has dimmed recently. Los Angeles's central business district's office market's vacancy increased to 10.4% in the first quarter from 9.8% the year earlier, though it is outperforming some harder hit markets like Orange County, where vacancy rates stood at 13.9%, according to CoStar Group.

Macquarie Office Trust is a publicly traded property trust managed by the Sydney-based Macquarie Group, a banking and financial-services company. It is facing some of the same stresses hitting its counterparts in the U.S. real-estate investment trust sector. The stock of the company, which owned about 42 properties in Australia, Europe, Japan and the U.S. as of December, has been battered as investors have been rattled by concerns about debt and falling real-estate values.

In December, Macquarie Office said it was undertaking a number of steps, including selling property, raising capital and reducing shareholder dividends to improve its balance sheet and shore up confidence. Those efforts have helped refinance or repay about 1.4 billion Australian dollars (US$1.04 billion) in debt that was to expire this year.

Adrian Taylor, chief executive of Macquarie Office Trust, said in an interview he expected that only one or two of the four properties it now has on the block in the U.S. will ultimately be sold. "We will sell assets where it makes sense but not at any price," Mr. Taylor said.

Macquarie is marketing the L.A. building as well as the Wells Fargo Center in Denver, Chase Tower in Indianapolis and a fourth building at 745 Atlantic Avenue in Boston.

Stress Tests Relax Credit Markets
By Kate Haywood
1186 words
6 May 2009
17:25
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

NEW YORK -- The rally in credit seemed relentless Wednesday as gains in U.S. stocks and an unexpected increase in pending U.S. home sales gave markets a boost ahead of the government's bank stress-test results.

Even as news reports pointed to significant capital needs by major banks ahead of the Thursday release of the results, investors appeared to be growing more confident about the viability of the financial sector.

Indeed, the cost of protection on a basket of highly rated North American corporate bonds fell to levels not seen over seven months as benchmark credit derivatives indices outpaced positive equity markets in a broad-based rally.

Bank of America Corp., Citigroup Inc. and Wells Fargo & Co. were among a number of large U.S. banks that need to raise more capital after the completion of the government's stress tests, according to people familiar with the matter and reports.

J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., American Express Co., Morgan Stanley, Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and Bank of New York Mellon Corp., however, reportedly won't need to raise more money to shore up their balance sheets.

The tests weren't restricted to traditional banks. GMAC LLC may have to raise as much as $11.5 billion of additional capital, according to news reports. However, MetLife Inc. isn't expected to need more capital.

Despite the leaks, there could still be some surprises for the market, according to Vivek Tawadey, head of credit portfolio strategy & head of credit research at BNP Paribas.

He said the extent of the need for capital could prove to be a negative for the markets.

It could be even more of a shock because it comes at a time "when most of the good news [both in terms of economic indicators and earnings] seem to have been priced in."

The National Association of Realtors' index for pending sales of previously owned homes, released Monday, rose 3.2% in March, while the Commerce Department said spending on construction projects that month rose by 0.3%, the first gain in six months.

Junk Bonds

New issuance remained the theme in the high-yield market, where issuers have lined up to take advantage of the sector's ongoing rally.

After pricing Tuesday afternoon, the $4.225 billion of new Teck Resources notes surged in secondary market trade, with all three tranches -- which priced between 94.65 and 95.25 cents on the dollar -- trading near par Wednesday, with some of the notes up as much as seven points, according to investors.

Goodyear is in the market with a planned seven-year note offering via joint bookrunners J.P. Morgan, Citigroup, Deutsche Bank and Goldman Sachs.

The Goodyear deal is now expected in the range of $1 billion, up from an initially announced $500 million, and the timetable has been accelerated with pricing expected later Wednesday, according to two investors familiar with the deal. Price talk is in the area of a five-point discount to par and an 11.5% yield. Proceeds will go toward general corporate purposes, including repayment of $500 million of senior floating-rate notes due Dec. 1, 2009.

Nalco Co. priced $500 million of senior notes at a discounted price of 97.86 cents on the dollar to yield 8.625%. Proceeds from the offering, along with proceeds from a contemplated new term loan facility, will go toward repaying term loans outstanding under the company's existing senior secured credit facilities.

Bonds traded higher in the secondary market, boosted by gains in lower-rated sectors such as auto parts makers and casinos. Gains in the high-yield CDX index, meanwhile, outpaced cash bonds, with the index up 1.6 points to 81.1, according to Markit.

Residential Capital's senior unsecured bonds were sharply higher -- by as much as 12 points -- even though it became apparent that ResCap parent GMAC would need to raise more capital.

Thomas Ferguson, analyst at KDP Investment Advisors, said that the reason behind the run-up wasn't completely clear as there hasn't been any particularly compelling news for ResCap.

Investment-Grade Bonds

The U.S. investment-grade corporate bond market saw a solid day of new bonds totaling around $5 billion, including $2 billion from General Electric Capital Corp., the financing arm of General Electric Co. These notes, unlike the bulk of deals from the company since autumn, didn't carry a guarantee from the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp.

"The market is favorable and there is demand for our paper," said spokesman Russell Wilkerson.

Proceeds of the deal will go toward the company's 2010 funding plan, he said. Underwriters on the deal are Barclays Capital, Goldman Sachs, HSBC, Morgan Stanley and Credit Suisse, according to another person familiar with the deal.

Financial companies have relied heavily on the FDIC's guarantee program to sell debt at cheap rates since it started in the fall. But a few have gone outside the program, particularly in recent weeks, to sell non-guaranteed debt.

General Electric Capital was one of the first companies to test the waters, and has sold $6.4 billion in non-FDIC backed debt since November, according to data provider Dealogic. During the same period, it sold $39.4 billion in guaranteed notes.

Other issuers Wednesday included Rabobank Nederland, Husky Energy and Xerox.

Meanwhile, bank credit-default swaps tightened significantly as investors grew more confident that the stress tests won't result in any haircuts, or reductions, for bondholders.

The benchmark high-grade credit derivatives index, the Markit CDX IG12 is quoted at 144 basis points by CMA DataVision. It closed at 157 basis points Tuesday, according to Markit.

Agency Mortgage-Backed Securities

Agency mortgage-backed securities continued to firm up, though at a much more modest pace than in the past couple of days. Risk premiums were two basis points tighter over Treasurys yields.

The Federal Reserve bought more of these securities than was available in the market. Also, investors expect prepayment speeds in April, to be announced Wednesday evening, to come in at 15%, a pace that indicates that homeowners aren't repaying as fast as was expected in the current refinancing regime.

Also, the refinance index has dropped to the low 5,000 levels for two straight weeks in a row, throwing off market participants' expectations.

Asset-Backed Securities

Information on how much the Fed dispersed to investors who applied for $10.6 billion in loans under its Term Asset-Backed Securities Loan Facility, or TALF, program in the third round of such funding, which ended Tuesday, will be released May 14. The fourth loan application deadline is June 2. Issuance picks up around the loan application deadline and market participants are expecting the next round to be mammoth. This time around, issuance of consumer loan-backed bonds reached $13.5 billion ahead of the Tuesday deadline.

Encouraging Signs Seen in Housing, Construction

Increases in housing-market activity and construction spending were the latest signs of a brighter outlook for the U.S. economy.

Pending sales of existing homes rose in March for the second-straight month, the National Association of Realtors said Monday. It marked the first back-to-back gains in that index in nearly a year, and the latest hopeful sign for the beleaguered housing market. Pending sales serve as a leading gauge, as they are based on contracts signed but not yet closed, a process that typically takes a month or two.

Activity was strongest in the South and West in March, the Realtors' group said, as the index overall increased 3.2 points from February to a level of 84.6. Sales of new and existing homes have fallen sharply in the past year, but have shown signs of life in recent months as falling prices and low mortgage rates help to make homes more affordable.

Meanwhile, the Commerce Department said Monday that spending on construction projects rose in March by 0.3% to $969 billion, the first gain in six months. Spending was down on residential construction but strong for nonresidential projects, particularly power plants and government structures, possibly reflecting a jump-start from the onset of the government's distribution of $787 billion of stimulus funds.

While both reports are encouraging, plenty of clouds remain on the horizon -- rising unemployment, in particular. The nation's 8.5% jobless rate is expected to jump toward 9% when April figures are released Friday by the Labor Department, and to flirt with double digits as layoffs continue to mount.

Nomura Securities chief economist David Resler wrote in a note to clients about construction spending: "We [still] expect that these expenditures will remain under considerable pressure this year due to rising vacancies and difficult financing conditions."

But the increase does suggest that the economy contracted at a slower rate than initially reported during the January-March period. The government last week reported that the economy contracted at a 6.1% annual rate in the first quarter. Morgan Stanley economists said they expect that figure will be revised upward to a drop of 5.9%, and also raised their forecast for the current quarter to a drop of 3.3% from the 3.5% projected prior to Monday's reports.

S&P Up for 2009 as Stocks Surge
By Peter A. McKay and Rob Curran

English
Copyright 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

An across-the-board surge in stocks turned the broad Standard & Poor's 500 positive for 2009 after data showed increased construction spending and pending-home sales.

Markets have been marching higher for nearly two months, with the S&P 500 gaining 34% since hitting its bear-market lows in early March. Data Monday that showed the first increase in construction spending in six months and a 3.2% jump in pending home sales gave stocks another shove.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average gained 214.33 points, or 2.6%, to end at 8426.74, the highest close since Jan. 13. The blue-chip benchmark is now down just 4% for the year to date and has risen 29% since closing at a 12-year nadir of 6547.05 on March 9.

The S&P 500 jumped 29.72 points, or 3.4%, to 907.24 as a late burst of buying turned the benchmark positive for the year to date, up 0.4%. Its financial sector jumped 10%, and its basic-materials sector gained 5.6%. Investors snapped up energy and consumer stocks, as well betting that the economy bottomed out in the first quarter, when it shrank at a 6.1% seasonally adjusted annual rate.

Bill Frejlich, a futures broker at Fox Investments in Chicago, said that there has been a marked change in investor sentiment.

"We've reached a point where the selloffs are short-lived," he said. "Even if you get a downdraft, it lasts a week or so, then you get a new high because people are looking for entry points to the market."

In another sign of the economic optimism sweeping trading floors, crude-oil futures settled at $54.47 a barrel, the highest level this year. Crude has gained more than 9% in the last four trading days and is up 60% from its 2009 closing low of $33.98, hit on February 12, 2009.

With stocks substantially above their worst levels for the current bear market, market watchers said that some investors were now feeling compelled to push funds into the market after missing out on the early part of the rally.

"If you're one of the big bears out there, you really have to worry that you haven't participated," said Robert Pavlik, chief market strategist at Banyan Partners in New York. "You're seriously underperforming at this point."

Joe Kinahan, chief derivatives strategist at brokerage thinkorswim, said many traders had expected the S&P 500 to pull back after hitting the 870 level, but that hasn't happened. "A lot of people are getting caught short here," he said.

The Nasdaq Composite Index, which is up 12% for the year to date and has led the recent recovery, climbed 44.36 points, or 2.6%, to 1763.56. The Russell 2000 rose 19.84 points, or 4.1% to 506.82. It too is now positive for the year to date, up 1.5%.

Financial stocks bounced, with Citigroup rising 7.7% and Bank of America up 19% ahead of the expected release later this week of the results of the U.S. government's stress tests of key banks. Bank of America denied a report that it's seeking to raise another $10 billion in capital. Wells Fargo surged 24% despite an Associated Press report that it was told by regulators it needed more capital.

Metals and mining stocks, which are particularly sensitive to changes in the economic outlook, jumped. Alcoa gained 6.9%, U.S. Steel rose 10% and Nucor gained 8.3%. Freeport-McMoran Copper & Gold gained 9.4%.

Airline stocks rose on hopes that travel demand would withstand the swine flu outbreak. The World Health Organization moved closer to declaring the new strain a global pandemic, but health officials cautioned that such a move doesn't mean the disease, which has proven mild outside of Mexico, is deadly to most people or will sweep the entire globe.

Delta Air Lines shares advanced 9.4%, Continental Airlines rose 18% and Southwest Airlines climbed 4.5%. Cruise-ship operator Carnival gained 13%.

Stocks were generally stronger overseas, with markets in Asia climbing after data showed an improvement in Chinese manufacturing activity. Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index jumped 5.5%, reclaiming the 16,000-point level for the first time since mid-October. Markets in Japan were closed for a holiday. In Europe, stocks made modest gains. Markets in the U.K. were also closed for a holiday.

Rich Golinski, a principal at the San Francisco-based wealth-management firm Bingham, Osborn & Scarborough, said he's concerned that a bout of euphoria has taken hold of the market since the bear-market lows and is due to end soon.

Referring to the worst-case expectations for corporate profits, which are still declining even if they've beaten analysts' expectations, he said: "There's a relief right now that the world isn't coming to an end, but a lot of serious problems are still around."

He said a worse-than-expected unemployment reading Friday could act as a catalyst for a prolonged market reversal. But the bigger risk may be that investors' mood simply swings back to more gloomy terrain on its own.

"You get the sense that it's not necessarily an economic data point that will get investors to refocus on the current difficulties," Mr. Golinski said.

U.S. News: Fewer Banks Tighten Their Lending Standards

(Copyright (c) 2009, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Although credit conditions remain strained, an April survey of loan officers by the Federal Reserve found a smaller number of banks were continuing to tighten loan standards, compared with a few months ago.

Glimmers of improvement were most notable in commercial lending. The Fed said 40% of the 53 domestic banks it surveyed between March 31 and April 14 said they tightened standards on commercial and industrial loans, a smaller percentage than the 65% that said in January that they tightened standards.

"The April survey marks the first time since January 2008 that the proportion of banks reporting such tightening fell below 50%," the central bank said. When banks tighten standards, they make it harder to get a loan by toughening certain criteria, such as for income, cash flow or indebtedness.

In the period, banks were also a little less aggressive about demanding more for the loans they actually made. Some 80% said they toughened terms on loans -- for instance, increasing the interest rate -- when compared with some benchmark. That was down from the 95% that said in January they demanded a higher rate.

About 65% of banks said they tightened standards for commercial real-estate loans, down from 80% in January. It was the first time since October 2007 that the percentage of banks tightening standards on commercial real-estate loans fell below 70%.

Consumer lending was mixed. While there was a greater percentage of banks tightening criteria for mortgage loans, the percentage doing so for home equity and certain other consumer loans eased a bit.

Meanwhile, increases in housing-market activity and construction spending were the latest signs of a brighter outlook for the economy.

Pending sales of existing homes rose in March for the second straight month, the National Association of Realtors said Monday. It marked the first back-to-back gains in that index in nearly a year, and the latest hopeful sign for the housing market. Pending sales serve as a leading gauge, as they are based on contracts signed but not yet closed.

The Commerce Department said Monday that spending on construction projects rose 0.3% to $969 billion in March, the first gain in six months. Spending was down on residential construction but strong for nonresidential projects.

U.S. construction spending increases --- First rise in 6 months; forecasting gauge of home sales also up
By Maya Jackson Randall and Meena Thiruvengadam
457 words
5 May 2009
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
M2
English
(c) 2009 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

WASHINGTON -- Total U.S. construction spending rose for the first time in six months in March, beating analyst expectations as public-sector outlays increased.

Separately, a forecasting gauge of home sales rose more than expected in March, buoyed by first-time home buyers taking advantage of lower prices.

U.S. construction spending rose 0.3% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $969.7 billion during the month, the Commerce Department reported Monday. Analysts had expected construction spending would fall by 1.3% in March.

U.S. construction spending has been falling since September, taking with it thousands of construction jobs. It has fallen more than 11% on a year-over-year basis.

In revising February's construction-spending estimates, the Commerce Department on Monday said spending fell 1.0%, slightly more than its original estimate of a 0.9% drop.

In March, residential-construction spending fell 4.1% to $265.85 billion. Residential spending fell a revised 5.6% in February, up from the Commerce Department's original estimate of a 4.1% drop. It was down 33.3% from March 2008.

Spending in the nonresidential sector rose 2.0% in March as spending for educational and power facilities rose while spending on office construction remained fairly steady.

Private-sector construction spending fell 0.1% in March to $661.0 billion and a revised 2.1% in February.

Public-sector construction spending by state and federal governments rose 1.1% in March to $308.7 billion. Public-sector spending was up a revised 1.3% in February.

The National Association of Realtors' index for pending sales of previously owned homes rose 3.2% to 84.6 from a revised 82.0 in February, the industry group said Monday.

Private analysts had projected that pending sales would grow 1.0% during March.

Still, Lawrence Yun, the association's chief economist, said it is too early to declare a recovery in the housing market.

"This increase could be the leading edge of first-time buyers responding to very favorable affordability conditions and an $8,000 tax credit, which increases buying power even more in areas where special programs allow buyers to use it as a down payment," Mr. Yun said in a statement. "We need several months of sustained growth to demonstrate a recovery in housing, which is necessary for the overall economy to turn around."

The pending-sales index, based on signed contracts for previously owned homes, was 1.1% above the level of 83.7 in March 2008.

D.R. Horton Inc.'s fiscal second-quarter loss narrowed as the home builder said market conditions remain challenging as inventories surge amid rising foreclosures unemployment.

Shares of D.R. Horton dropped 3.9% to $12.97 in after-hours trading as results fell short of estimates. Shares have soared 90% in 2009.

Home builders, which have posted a string of losses, have seen their turnaround efforts mired by tighter credit practices and poor consumer confidence. The industry has struggled to unload inventory, as many builders compete with bargain-priced foreclosed homes, and have pinned their hope on the success of stimulus initiatives. Meanwhile, companies have written off billions of dollars in prior spending and assets as values slumped.

But optimism is mounting that the bottom is near. The National Association of Realtors reported Monday that its pending Home Sales Index increased, helped as property prices fall, more than 50% from their peak in some markets.

For the quarter ended March 31, D.R. Horton reported a loss of $108.6 million, or 34 cents a share, compared with a year-ago loss of $1.31 billion, or $4.14 a share. The latest results included $48.1 million in pretax charges, compared with $834.1 million in the prior year.

Revenue slumped 52% to $775.3 million.

Analysts polled by Thomson Reuters projected a per-share loss of 30 cents on revenue of $817 million.

Closings fell 47% to 3,585 homes. At March 31, the company's sales backlog of homes under contract was 4,581 valued at $963 million, down from 8,947 and $2.1 billion, respectively. Net sales orders fell 45% to 4,160 homes, and the cancellation rate was 30%.

The company said as of March 31, it was in compliance with all covenants associated with its home building revolving credit facility and had $275 million available under its borrowing base. The company said it doesn't anticipate having to borrow from the facility for the remainder of its term ending in December 2011, so the company decided to terminate the facility effective May 11, which should save over $3 million a year in nonuse fees.

D.R. Horton has long been the nation's largest residential builder by volume, but Pulte Homes Inc.'s proposed combination with Centex Corp. will usurp D.R. Horton's No. 1 position.
